There are policies that help or hurt, but great minds have been trying to figure this out for a really long time. I remember the shock of the closing mills and plants in the Eighties. We‚Äôve talked tax credits, retraining, marriage, family, education for generations. /17
We know how people can enjoy upward mobility. We don‚Äôt know how government can systematically improve the mobility of people who don‚Äôt finish their education and have children out of wedlock. And we don‚Äôt know how government can motivate the success sequence at scale.  /16
So we have a number of HUGE changes in American cultural life, one of which is a collapse in marriage culture that is self-defeating and helps make poverty stickier, and the populist says that elites are to blame and government can fix it. This is just flat-out wrong. /15
We can‚Äôt forget that our upper-middle-class is growing (something I mention in my piece), and one of the reasons why it grows is that of course people from lower economic classes who demonstrate certain core disciplines enter the upper-middle-class at scale. /14
For example, here‚Äôs the illustrious @WilcoxNMP noting that poor millennials who follow the ‚Äúsuccess sequence‚Äù are upwardly mobile. /13 https://t.co/xs3NPgUqp0
Who was most able to respond and succeed in this environment? People who resisted the siren call of sexual revolution excess, and who dug in and finished at the very least their high school education. In fact, these qualities lead to considerable upward mobility. /12
So we get a shifting workforce because of civil rights/feminism, a more competitive manufacturing environment, changing personal morality, and immense growth in new industries that require different skill sets. /11
On top of this, we get a sexual revolution. Yes, that had immense elite backing, but it was adopted with extreme enthusiasm across social classes and over the strenuous objections of Protestant/Catholic religious leaders who sounded warning bells as loudly as they could. /10
(My family bought Dodge cars in the 1970s, and they were horrifyingly bad. I‚Äôll never forget the amazement of buying a Toyota and never having to take it to the shop.) /9
Oh, and we also can‚Äôt forget that some American manufacturers (in all their immense power) became complacent and arrogant. They made inferior products and then screamed ‚Äúbuy American‚Äù when families purchased products that were best for their lives. /8
This isn‚Äôt elite spite or indifference. It‚Äôs economic reality. Even the best policies would have merely tinkered at the edges of these tectonic shifts. /7
Productivity gains also meant that we could (and do) produce more with less. But if we didn‚Äôt become more productive we‚Äôd even be less competitive. Simply put, we could not possibly freeze in amber the golden age of American manufacturing. /6
As for the previous ascendancy of American manufacturing, never forget we gained immense strength as many of the world‚Äôs most advanced societies were in ruins after WWII. When they recovered, they became extremely competitive. /5.
The civil rights revolution (a bottom-up movement) changed the nation in good ways, but it‚Äôs still a change. Feminism changed the nation in good and bad ways. The Information Age changed the nature of skills and training necessary to be economically competitive /4.
